<p><b>James Clerk Maxwell</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:100%; font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Fellow of the Royal Society of Edinburgh">FRSE</a> <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (13 June 1831 – 5 November 1879) was a Scottish <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mactutor_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-mactutor-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a> responsible for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_theory" title="Classical theory">classical theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, which was the first theory to describe electricity, <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> and light as different manifestations of the same phenomenon. <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell's equations</a> for electromagnetism have been called the "<a href="/wiki/Unification_(physics)#Unification_of_magnetism,_electricity,_light_and_related_radiation" title="Unification (physics)">second great unification in physics</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Unification_(physics)#Unification_of_gravity_and_astronomy" title="Unification (physics)">the first one</a> had been realised by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>.
</p><p>With the publication of "<a href="/wiki/A_Dynamical_Theory_of_the_Electromagnetic_Field" title="A Dynamical Theory of the Electromagnetic Field">A Dynamical Theory of the Electromagnetic Field</a>" in 1865, Maxwell demonstrated that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_force" title="Electric force">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> travel through space as <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> moving at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.  He proposed that light is an undulation in the same medium that is the cause of electric and magnetic phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADTEF_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADTEF-4">[4]</a></sup> The unification of light and electrical phenomena led his prediction of the existence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a>. Maxwell is also regarded as a founder of the modern field of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>He helped develop the <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution</a>, a statistical means of describing aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic theory of gases</a>. He is also known for presenting the first durable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_photograph" title="Colour photograph">colour photograph</a> in 1861 and for his foundational work on analysing the <a href="/wiki/Structural_rigidity" title="Structural rigidity">rigidity</a> of rod-and-joint frameworks (<a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">trusses</a>) like those in many bridges.
</p><p>His discoveries helped usher in the era of modern physics, laying the foundation for such fields as <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Many physicists regard Maxwell as the 19th-century scientist having the greatest influence on 20th-century physics. His contributions to the science are considered by many to be of the same magnitude as those of Isaac Newton and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In the millennium poll—a survey of the 100 most prominent physicists—Maxwell was voted the third greatest physicist of all time, behind only Newton and Einstein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> On the centenary of Maxwell's birthday, Einstein described Maxwell's work as the "most profound and the most fruitful that physics has experienced since the time of Newton".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Einstein, when he visited the University of Cambridge in 1922, was told by his host that he had done great things because he stood on Newton's shoulders; Einstein replied: "No I don't. I stand on the shoulders of Maxwell."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
